story\"><div id=\"story\" class=\"jsx-4020579012 storyTopperMediaVertical\"><div class=\"jsx-4020579012 storyTopperTextCol\"><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 storyTopperText vertical \"><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 hedWrap\"><h3 style=\"color:#7D38A3\" class=\"jsx-2229707591 eyebrow\"><a href=\"/artificial-intelligence/\" style=\"color:#7D38A3\" class=\"jsx-2229707591\">Artificial Intelligence</a></h3><h1 class=\"jsx-2229707591 hed\">What AI still can\u2019t do</h1></div><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 dekWrap\"><h2 class=\"jsx-2229707591 dek\">Artificial intelligence won\u2019t be very smart if computers don\u2019t grasp cause and effect. That\u2019s something even humans have trouble with.</h2><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 meta\"><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 byline\">by<!-- --> <a href=\"/profile/brian-bergstein/\" class=\"jsx-3495602202 \" style=\"font-weight: 600; text-decoration: none; color: rgb(0, 0, 0);\">Brian Bergstein</a></div><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 date\">Feb 19, 2020</div></div></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-4020579012 storyTopperMedia\"><div style=\"position:relative\"><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 mediaContainer\"><picture style=\"display:block;width:100%;height:auto\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 enlargable\"><source media=\"(max-width: 959px\" srcset=\"data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7 1w\" class=\"jsx-1546557212\"><source media=\"(min-width: 960px\" srcset=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=550&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=1100&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 2x\" class=\"jsx-1546557212\"><img alt=\"causality bleed\" style=\"display:block;width:100%;height:auto\" src=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=550&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941\" srcset=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=550&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=1100&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 2x\" role=\"button\" tabindex=\"0\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 enlargable\"></picture><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 clickToEnlarge\"></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-4020579012 storyTopperMediaCaptionWrapper\"><div class=\"jsx-4020579012 storyTopperMediaCaption\"></div><div class=\"jsx-4020579012 storyTopperMediaCredit\">Saiman Chow</div></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-3547280565 storyContainer\"><div class=\"jsx-3547280565 storyMain\" style=\"overflow: hidden;\"><div class=\"jsx-3547280565 storyWell\"><div class=\"jsx-422929121 storyTopper vertical\"><div class=\"jsx-422929121 storyTopperTextContainer vertical\"><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 storyTopperText  \"><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 hedWrap\"><h3 style=\"color:#7D38A3\" class=\"jsx-2229707591 eyebrow\"><a href=\"/artificial-intelligence/\" style=\"color:#7D38A3\" class=\"jsx-2229707591\">Artificial Intelligence</a></h3><h1 class=\"jsx-2229707591 hed\">What AI still can\u2019t do</h1></div><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 dekWrap\"><h2 class=\"jsx-2229707591 dek\">Artificial intelligence won\u2019t be very smart if computers don\u2019t grasp cause and effect. That\u2019s something even humans have trouble with.</h2><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 meta\"><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 byline\">by<!-- --> <a href=\"/profile/brian-bergstein/\" class=\"jsx-3495602202 \" style=\"font-weight: 600; text-decoration: none; color: rgb(0, 0, 0);\">Brian Bergstein</a></div><div class=\"jsx-2229707591 date\">Feb 19, 2020</div></div></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-422929121 storyTopperMediaMobile vertical\"><div style=\"position:relative\"><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 mediaContainer\"><picture style=\"display:block;width:100%;height:auto\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 enlargable\"><source media=\"(max-width: 959px\" srcset=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=959&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=1918&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 2x\" class=\"jsx-1546557212\"><source media=\"(min-width: 960px\" srcset=\"data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7 1w\" class=\"jsx-1546557212\"><img alt=\"causality bleed\" style=\"display:block;width:100%;height:auto\" src=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=959&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941\" srcset=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=959&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/causality-bleed.png?sw=1918&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=2206&amp;ch=2941 2x\" role=\"button\" tabindex=\"0\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 enlargable\"></picture><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 clickToEnlarge\"></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-422929121 storyTopperMediaMobileCaptionWrapper\"><div class=\"jsx-422929121 storyTopperMediaMobileCaption\"></div><div class=\"jsx-422929121 storyTopperMediaMobileCredit\">Saiman Chow</div></div></div></div><div style=\"min-height:250px\" class=\"showAd\"><div style=\"margin: 0px auto; padding: 0px 0px 20px; width: 300px;\"><div style=\"width: 300px; height: 250px;\"></div></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-632160040 storyBody\"><div class=\"jsx-632160040 storyContent\" style=\"max-height: 525.849px;\"><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">I</span><span class=\"s2\">n</span><span class=\"s1\"> less than a decade, computers have become extremely good at diagnosing diseases, translating languages, and transcribing speech. They can outplay humans at complicated strategy games, create photorealistic images, and suggest useful replies to your emails. </span></span></p><aside class=\"jsx-94352816 magazine\"><a href=\"/magazine/2020/03/\" class=\"jsx-94352816 thumbnail\"><div style=\"background-color:transparent;position:relative;padding-bottom:136.54984069185252%;height:0\"><div><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 mediaContainer\"><img alt=\"\" src=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/ma20cover-formag.jpg?sw=172\" srcset=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/ma20cover-formag.jpg?sw=172 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/ma20cover-formag.jpg?sw=344 2x\" tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 \" style=\"display: block; width: 100%; height: auto; position: absolute; top: 0px; left: 0px;\"></div></div></div></a><h5 class=\"jsx-94352816 title\">This story is part of our<!-- --> <span>March/April 2020</span> <!-- -->issue</h5><a href=\"/magazine/2020/03/\" class=\"jsx-94352816 magazineLink\"><span class=\"jsx-94352816 hover\">See the rest of the issue</span><i class=\"jsx-94352816 icon ion-ios-arrow-right\"></i></a><br class=\"jsx-94352816\"><a href=\"/subscribe/\" class=\"jsx-94352816 magazineLink\"><span class=\"jsx-94352816 hover\">Subscribe</span><i class=\"jsx-94352816 icon ion-ios-arrow-right\"></i></a></aside><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">Yet despite these impressive achievements, artificial intelligence has glaring weaknesses. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">Machine-learning systems can be duped or confounded by situations they haven\u2019t seen before. A self-driving car gets flummoxed by a scenario that a human driver could handle easily. An AI system laboriously trained to carry out one task (identifying cats, say) has to be taught all over again to do something else (identifying dogs). In the process, it\u2019s liable to lose some of the expertise it had in the original task. Computer scientists call this problem \u201ccatastrophic forgetting.\u201d </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">These shortcomings have something in common: they exist because AI systems don\u2019t understand causation. They see that some events are associated with other events, but they don\u2019t ascertain which things directly make other things happen. It\u2019s as if you knew that the presence of clouds made rain likelier, but you didn\u2019t know clouds caused rain.</span></span></p><figure class=\"jsx-1143656047 imageset inbody\"><div class=\"jsx-2418869611 image inbody\"><div style=\"background-color:transparent;position:relative;padding-bottom:29.5%;height:0\"><div><noscript><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 mediaContainer\"><img alt=\"Elias Bareinboim\" style=\"display:block;width:100%;height:auto;position:absolute;top:0;left:0\" src=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/pic-bareinboim-web1_0.jpg?sw=616&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=3000&amp;ch=885\" srcSet=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/pic-bareinboim-web1_0.jpg?sw=616&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=3000&amp;ch=885 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/pic-bareinboim-web1_0.jpg?sw=1232&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=3000&amp;ch=885 2x\" role=\"button\" tabindex=\"0\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 enlargable\"/><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 clickToEnlarge\"></div></div></noscript></div></div></div><figcaption class=\"jsx-4039366481 caption inbody\">Elias Bareinboim: AI systems are clueless when it comes to causation.</figcaption></figure><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">Understanding cause and effect is a big aspect of what we call common sense, and it\u2019s an area in which AI systems today \u201care clueless,\u201d says Elias Bareinboim. He should know: as the director of the new Causal Artificial Intelligence Lab at Columbia University, he\u2019s at the forefront of efforts to fix this problem. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">His idea is to infuse artificial-intelligence research with insights from the relatively new science of causality, a field shaped to a huge extent by Judea Pearl, a Turing Award\u2013winning scholar who considers Bareinboim his prot\u00e9g\u00e9. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">As Bareinboim and Pearl describe it, AI\u2019s ability to spot correlations\u2014e.g., that clouds make rain more likely\u2014is merely the simplest level of causal reasoning. It\u2019s good enough to have driven the boom in the AI technique known as deep learning over the past decade. Given a great deal of data about familiar situations, this method can lead to very good predictions. A computer can calculate the probability that a patient with certain symptoms has a certain disease, because it has learned just how often thousands or even millions of other people with the same symptoms had that disease. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">But there\u2019s a growing consensus that progress in AI will stall if computers don\u2019t get better at wrestling with causation. If machines could grasp that certain things lead to other things, they wouldn\u2019t have to learn everything anew all the time\u2014they could take what they had learned in one domain and apply it to another. And if machines could use common sense we\u2019d be able to put more trust in them to take actions on their own, knowing that they aren\u2019t likely to make dumb errors. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s1\">Today\u2019s AI has only a limited ability to infer what will result from a given action. In reinforcement learning, a technique that has allowed machines to master games like chess and Go, a system uses extensive trial and error to discern which moves will essentially cause them to win. But this approach doesn\u2019t work in messier settings in the real world. It doesn\u2019t even leave a machine with </span><span class=\"s3\">a general understanding of how it might play other games. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">An even higher level of causal thinking would be the ability to reason about why things happened and ask \u201cwhat if\u201d questions. A patient dies while in a clinical trial; was it the fault of the experimental medicine or something else? School test scores are falling; what policy changes would most improve them? This kind of reasoning is far beyond the current capability of artificial intelligence. </span></span></p><div><h3 class=\"p4\"><span class=\"s4\">Performing miracles</span></h3></div><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\">The dream of endowing computers with causal reasoning drew Bareinboim from Brazil to the United States in 2008, after he completed a master\u2019s in computer science at the Federal University of Rio de Janeiro. He jumped at an opportunity to study under Judea Pearl, a computer scientist and statistician at UCLA. Pearl, 83, is a giant\u2014<em>the</em> giant\u2014of causal inference, and his career helps illustrate why it\u2019s hard to create AI that understands causality.</span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">Even well-trained scientists are apt to misinterpret correlations as signs of causation\u2014or to err in the opposite direction, hesitating to call out causation even when it\u2019s justified. In the 1950s, for example, a few prominent statisticians muddied the waters around whether tobacco caused cancer. They argued that without an experiment randomly assigning people to be smokers or nonsmokers, no one could rule out the possibility that some unknown\u2014stress, perhaps, or some gene\u2014caused people both to smoke and to get lung cancer.</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">Eventually, the fact that smoking causes cancer was definitively established, but it needn\u2019t have taken so long. Since then, Pearl and other statisticians have devised a mathematical approach to identifying what facts would be required to support a causal claim. Pearl\u2019s method shows that, given the prevalence of smoking and lung cancer, an independent factor causing both would be extremely unlikely.</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">Conversely, Pearl\u2019s formulas also help identify when correlations can\u2019t be used to determine causation. Bernhard Sch\u00f6lkopf, who researches causal AI techniques as a director at Germany\u2019s Max Planck Institute for Intelligent Systems, points out that you can predict a country\u2019s birth rate if you know its population of storks. That isn\u2019t because storks deliver babies or because babies attract storks, but probably because economic development leads to more babies and more storks. Pearl has helped give statisticians and computer scientists ways of attacking such problems, Sch\u00f6lkopf says.</span></span></p><figure class=\"jsx-1143656047 imageset inbody\"><div class=\"jsx-2418869611 image inbody\"><div style=\"background-color:transparent;position:relative;padding-bottom:29.5%;height:0\"><div><noscript><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 mediaContainer\"><img alt=\"Judea Pearl\" style=\"display:block;width:100%;height:auto;position:absolute;top:0;left:0\" src=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/judeapearl-web1.jpg?sw=616&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=3000&amp;ch=885\" srcSet=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/judeapearl-web1.jpg?sw=616&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=3000&amp;ch=885 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/judeapearl-web1.jpg?sw=1232&amp;cx=0&amp;cy=0&amp;cw=3000&amp;ch=885 2x\" role=\"button\" tabindex=\"0\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 enlargable\"/><div class=\"jsx-1546557212 clickToEnlarge\"></div></div></noscript></div></div></div><figcaption class=\"jsx-4039366481 caption inbody\">Judea Pearl: His theory of causal reasoning has transformed science.</figcaption></figure><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">Pearl\u2019s work has also led to the development of causal Bayesian networks\u2014software that sifts through large amounts of data to detect which variables appear to have the most influence on other variables. For example, GNS Healthcare, a company in Cambridge, Massachusetts, uses these techniques to advise researchers about experiments that look promising. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">In one project, GNS worked with researchers who study multiple myeloma, a kind of blood cancer. The researchers wanted to know why some patients with the disease live longer than others after getting stem-cell transplants, a common form of treatment. The software churned through data with 30,000 variables and pointed to a few that seemed especially likely to be causal. Biostatisticians and experts in the disease zeroed in on one in particular: the level of a certain protein in patients\u2019 bodies. Researchers could then run a targeted clinical trial to see whether patients with the protein did indeed benefit more from the treatment. \u201cIt\u2019s way faster than poking here and there in the lab,\u201d says GNS cofounder Iya Khalil.</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">Nonetheless, the improvements that Pearl and other scholars have achieved in causal theory haven\u2019t yet made many inroads in deep learning, which identifies correlations without too much worry about causation. Bareinboim is working to take the next step: making computers more useful tools for human causal explorations.</span></span></p><aside class=\"jsx-3269038538 l-pullquote--text-col pullquote-style--default pullquote-size--default\"><p class=\"jsx-3269038538 pullquote__text pullquote__text--quote\">Pearl says AI can\u2019t be truly intelligent until it has a rich understanding of cause and effect, which would enable the introspection that is at the core of cognition.</p></aside><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">One of his systems, which is still in beta, can help scientists determine whether they have sufficient data to answer a causal question. Richard McElreath, an anthropologist at the Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology, is using the software to guide research into why humans go through menopause (we are the only apes that do).</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">The hypothesis is that the decline of fertility in older women benefited early human societies because women who put more effort into caring for grandchildren ultimately had more descendants. But what evidence might exist today to support the claim that children do better with grandparents around? Anthropologists can\u2019t just compare the educational or medical outcomes of children who have lived with grandparents and those who haven\u2019t. There are what statisticians call confounding factors: grandmothers might be likelier to live with grandchildren who need the most help. Bareinboim\u2019s software can help McElreath discern which studies about kids who grew up with their grandparents are least riddled with confounding factors and could be valuable in answering his causal query. \u201cIt\u2019s a huge step forward,\u201d McElreath says.</span></span></p><div><h3 class=\"p4\"><span class=\"s4\">The last mile</span></h3></div><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">B</span><span class=\"s3\">areinboim talks fast and often gestures with two hands in the air, as if he\u2019s trying to balance two sides of a mental equation. It was halfway through the semester when I visited him at Columbia in October, but it seemed as if he had barely moved into his office\u2014hardly anything on the walls, no books on the shelves, only a sleek Mac computer and a whiteboard so dense with equations and diagrams that it looked like a detail from a cartoon about a mad professor. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">He shrugged off the provisional state of the room, saying he had been very busy giving talks about both sides of the causal revolution. Bareinboim believes work like his offers the opportunity not just to incorporate causal thinking into machines, but also to improve it in humans. </span></span></p><aside class=\"jsx-2623490083 signup-wrapper\"><aside class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 newsletter-signup\"><div class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 intro\"><div class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 icon\"><svg xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/2000/svg\" viewBox=\"0 0 43.54 55.59\" style=\"fill:#4a4a4a\"><polygon points=\"27.11 37.52 12.76 23.17 16.29 19.63 27.11 30.45 37.92 19.63 41.46 23.17 27.11 37.52\"></polygon><rect x=\"24.77\" y=\"3.79\" width=\"5\" height=\"30\"></rect><path d=\"M0,0H19.75c16.4,0,23.79,11.51,23.79,28s-7.93,27.6-24.34,27.6H0ZM19.05,51c13.29,0,19-8.94,19-23s-5.2-23.4-18.5-23.4H5.21V51Z\"></path><path d=\"M11.11,3.28h5.21V39.79H36.94v4.82H11.11Z\"></path></svg></div><div class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 copy\"><h4 class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 title\">Sign up for<!-- --> <strong class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940\">The Download</strong> \u2014 <!-- -->your daily dose of what's up in emerging technology</h4></div></div><form class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940\"><input type=\"email\" name=\"email\" required=\"\" placeholder=\"Enter your email\" class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 emailBox\"><div class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 marketingWrapper\"><div class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 marketingIntro\">Also stay updated on MIT Technology Review initiatives and events?</div><div class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 marketingRadios\"><label for=\"marketingYes_433334\" class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940\"><input type=\"radio\" name=\"marketing\" value=\"true\" required=\"\" id=\"marketingYes_433334\" class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 marketingRadio\">Yes</label><label for=\"marketingNo_433334\" class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940\"><input type=\"radio\" name=\"marketing\" value=\"false\" required=\"\" id=\"marketingNo_433334\" class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 marketingRadio\">No</label></div></div><input type=\"submit\" value=\"Sign up\" class=\"jsx-3784032550 jsx-3019503940 submitButton\"></form></aside></aside><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">Getting people to think more carefully about causation isn\u2019t necessarily much easier than teaching it to machines, he says. Researchers in a wide range of disciplines, from molecular biology to public policy, are sometimes content to unearth correlations that are not actually rooted in causal relationships. For instance, some studies suggest drinking alcohol will kill you early, while others indicate that moderate consumption is fine and even beneficial, and still other research has found that heavy drinkers outlive nondrinkers. This phenomenon, known as the \u201creproducibility crisis,\u201d crops up not only in medicine and nutrition but also in psychology and economics. \u201cYou can see the fragility of all these inferences,\u201d says Bareinboim. \u201cWe\u2019re flipping results every couple of years.\u201d </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">He argues that anyone asking \u201cwhat if\u201d\u2014medical researchers setting up clinical trials, social scientists developing pilot programs, even web publishers preparing A/B tests\u2014should start not merely by gathering data but by using Pearl\u2019s causal logic and software like Bareinboim\u2019s to determine whether the available data could possibly answer a causal hypothesis. Eventually, he envisions this leading to \u201cautomated scientist\u201d software: a human could dream up a causal question to go after, and the software would combine causal inference theory with machine-learning techniques to rule out experiments that wouldn\u2019t answer the question. That might save scientists from a huge number of costly dead ends.</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">Bareinboim described this vision while we were sitting in the lobby of MIT\u2019s Sloan School of Management, after a talk he gave last fall. \u201cWe have a building here at MIT with, I don\u2019t know, 200 people,\u201d he said. How do those social scientists, or any scientists anywhere, decide which experiments to pursue and which data points to gather? By following their intuition: \u201cThey are trying to see where things will lead, based on their current understanding.\u201d </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">That\u2019s an inherently limited approach, he said, because human scientists designing an experiment can consider only a handful of variables in their minds at once. A computer, on the other hand, can see the interplay of hundreds or thousands of variables. Encoded with \u201cthe basic principles\u201d of Pearl\u2019s causal calculus and able to calculate what might happen with new sets of variables, an automated scientist could suggest exactly which experiments the human researchers should spend their time on. Maybe some public policy that has been shown to work only in Texas could be made to work in California if a few causally relevant factors were better appreciated. Scientists would no longer be \u201cdoing experiments in the darkness,\u201d Bareinboim said. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">He also doesn\u2019t think it\u2019s that far off: \u201cThis is the last mile before the victory.\u201d</span></span></p><div><h3 class=\"p4\"><span class=\"s4\">What if? </span></h3></div><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">F</span><span class=\"s3\">inishing that mile will probably require techniques that are just beginning to be developed. For example, Yoshua Bengio, a computer scientist at the University of Montreal who shared the 2018 Turing Award for his work on deep learning, is trying to get neural networks\u2014the software at the heart of deep learning\u2014to do \u201cmeta-learning\u201d and notice the causes of things. </span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">As things stand now, if you wanted a neural network to detect when people are dancing, you\u2019d show it many, many images of dancers. If you wanted it to identify when people are running, you\u2019d show it many, many images of runners. The system would learn to distinguish runners from dancers by identifying features that tend to be different in the images, such as the positions of a person\u2019s hands and arms. But Bengio points out that fundamental knowledge about the world can be gleaned by analyzing the things that are similar or \u201cinvariant\u201d across data sets. Maybe a neural network could learn that movements of the legs physically cause both running and dancing. Maybe after seeing these examples and many others that show people only a few feet off the ground, a machine would eventually understand something about gravity and how it limits human movement. Over time, with enough meta-learning about variables that are consistent across data sets, a computer could gain causal knowledge that would be reusable in many domains.</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">For his part, Pearl says AI can\u2019t be truly intelligent until it has a rich understanding of cause and effect. Although causal reasoning wouldn\u2019t be sufficient for an artificial general intelligence, it\u2019s necessary, he says, because it would enable the introspection that is at the core of cognition. \u201cWhat if\u201d questions \u201care the building blocks of science, of moral attitudes, of free will, of consciousness,\u201d Pearl told me.</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"s3\">You can\u2019t draw Pearl into predicting how long it will take for computers to get powerful causal reasoning abilities. \u201cI am not a futurist,\u201d he says. But in any case, he thinks the first move should be to develop machine-learning tools that combine data with available scientific knowledge: \u201cWe have a lot of knowledge that resides in the human skull which is not utilized.\u201d</span></span></p><p class=\"jsx-671803276\"><span class=\"jsx-671803276\"><em>Brian Bergstein, a former editor at MIT Technology Review, is deputy opinion editor at the Boston Globe.</em></span><svg xmlns=\"http://www.w3.org/2000/svg\" viewBox=\"0 0 288 287.99\" class=\"jsx-671803276 glyph\"><path d=\"M199.49,66.67,133,133.11H66.56V66.67Zm44.31,0-66.46,66.44V354.66h66.46V133.11H354.56V66.67Z\" transform=\"translate(-66.56 -66.67)\" class=\"jsx-671803276\"></path></svg></p></div><div class=\"jsx-632160040 storyExpander\"><div class=\"jsx-632160040 storyExpanderGradient\"></div><div class=\"jsx-632160040 storyExpanderButtonWrapper\"><button type=\"button\" class=\"jsx-632160040 storyExpanderButton\"><span class=\"jsx-632160040 cta\">Read the whole story</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;<span class=\"jsx-632160040 wordsLeft\">2173 words</span></button></div></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-3547280565 sidebar\"><div class=\"jsx-3547280565 sidebarTop\"></div><div class=\"jsx-3547280565 sidebarBottom\"></div></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-3151335682 meter\"><div class=\"jsx-3151335682 meterContainer\"><div role=\"button\" tabindex=\"0\" class=\"jsx-3151335682 close\"><span class=\"jsx-3151335682 close-icon\">\u00d7</span></div><p class=\"jsx-3151335682 copy\">You've read <span class=\"jsx-3151335682 count\">1/3</span> of your free monthly feature stories. virus.</h3></div><a class=\"bodyImage \" aria-hidden=\"true\" tabindex=\"-1\" href=\"/s/615284/what-are-the-best-coronavirus-treatments/\"><div style=\"position: relative; padding-bottom: 66%; height: data-event-label=\"feed-unit-006\" href=\"/s/615290/how-to-prepare-for-the-coronavirus-covid19/\">How to prepare for the coronavirus like a pro</a></h2><h3 class=\"jsx-860405766 summary\">Some of the smartest people I know are getting ready for a crisis\u2014including me.</h3></div><div class=\"jsx-860405766 cta\"><a class=\"ctaLink\" data-event-category=\"story-page-feed\" data-event-action=\"click\" data-event-label=\"feed-unit-006\" href=\"/s/615290/how-to-prepare-for-the-coronavirus-covid19/\">Read more</a></div><div class=\"jsx-860405766 img\"><a class=\"\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 mediaContainer\"><img alt=\"Coronavirus prepper\" style=\"display:block;width:100%;height:auto;position:absolute;top:0;left:0\" src=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/prepper-521401357-web.jpg?sw=768&amp;cx=3&amp;cy=13&amp;cw=2975&amp;ch=1673\" srcSet=\"https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/prepper-521401357-web.jpg?sw=768&amp;cx=3&amp;cy=13&amp;cw=2975&amp;ch=1673 1x, https://cdn.technologyreview.com/i/images/prepper-521401357-web.jpg?sw=1536&amp;cx=3&amp;cy=13&amp;cw=2975&amp;ch=1673 2x\" tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"jsx-1546557212 \"/></div></noscript></div></div></a></div></article></div><div class=\"jsx-2366629197 feedUnitWrapper\"><article class=\"jsx-860405766 storyTease storyTease--expandable\"><div class=\"jsx-860405766 meta\"><div class=\"jsx-860405766 metaLeft\"><a class=\"eyebrowLink\" href=\"/space/\"><h4 class=\"jsx-860405766 eyebrow\" style=\"color: rgb(171, 7, 7);\">Space</h4></a><time datetime=\"2020-02-28T07:25:34-05:00\" title=\"2020-02-28T07:25:34-05:00\" class=\"jsx-2054328710 timestamp\">Feb 28</time></div><div class=\"jsx-860405766 metaShareMenu\"><div class=\"jsx-523952996 container\"><button type=\"button\" aria-label=\"Open share menu\" class=\"jsx-523952996 shareMenuToggle\"><div class=\"jsx-523952996 dot\"></div><div class=\"jsx-523952996 dot\"></div><div class=\"jsx-523952996 dot\"></div></button></div></div></div><div class=\"jsx-860405766 headWrap\"><h2 class=\"jsx-860405766 head\"><a class=\"headLink\" data-event-category=\"story-page-feed\" data-event-action=\"click\" data-event-label=\"feed-unit-007\" href=\"/f/615300/astronomers-have-detected-the-biggest-explosion-the-universe/\">Astronomers have detected the biggest explosion in the universe</a></h2></div><div class=\"jsx-860405766 expandableBody\"><div class=\"jsx-1279085685 expandableBody\"><p class=\"jsx-1279085685 textBeforeButton\"><strong>The news:</strong> Astronomers have detected the biggest explosion in space that\u2019s ever been observed. The massive eruption occurred in the Ophiuchus cluster, about 390 million light-years from Earth, where...</p><div class=\"jsx-1279085685 mainCopyContainer\" style=\"height: 0px; transition: height 250ms ease-in 0s;\"><div class=\"jsx-1279085685 mainCopy\"><p class=\"jsx-1279085685\"><strong>What is it?</strong> It is believed to have been caused by a supermassive black hole in the cluster\u2019s central galaxy, according to a new study in <span class=\"jsx-2473920458 storyLink\"><a class=\"\" href=\"https://arxiv.org/abs/2002.01291\">The Astrophysical Journal</a></span>. Black holes sometimes expel huge amounts of energy and matter in the form of two perpendicular jets. This particular event was so big it smashed a gigantic cavity in the gas around the black hole. This cavity is now filled with radio waves created by electrons that the jet had accelerated almost to the speed of light.</p><p class=\"jsx-1279085685\"><strong>How big?</strong> To give a sense of the scale of this weird hole in space, you could fit 15 Milky Way galaxies in a row into it, <span class=\"jsx-2473920458 storyLink\"><a class=\"\" href=\"https://chandra.si.edu/photo/2020/ophiuchus/\">says</a></span> lead author Simona Giacintucci, director of research at the Naval Research Laboratory in Washington, DC.&nbsp;The explosion is now over, according to the radio data.</p><p class=\"jsx-1279085685\"><span class=\"jsx-1279085685 textBeforeButton\"><strong>How it was discovered:</strong> The finding was made thanks to a combination of x-ray data from NASA\u2019s Chandra X-Ray Observatory, Europe\u2019s XMM-Newton space telescope, the Murchison Widefield Array in Australia, and the Giant Metrewave Telescope in India. Researchers noticed unusual activity in the Ophiuchus cluster <span class=\"jsx-2473920458 storyLink\"><a class=\"\" href=\"https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2016MNRAS.460.2752W/abstract\">back in 2016</a></span> using data from the Chandra telescope, but the cavity was so large, and would have required so much energy to form, that they initially didn\u2019t think it was feasible.&nbsp;</span></p><div class=\"jsx-702922605 storyFooter\"><div class=\"jsx-702922605 storyFooterContainer\"><div class=\"jsx-3334558213 footerShare\"><div class=\"jsx-3334558213 share\"><div That\u2019s something even humans have trouble with.\",\"byline\":[{\"element\":\"a\",\"attributes\":{\"href\":\"/profile/brian-bergstein/\"},\"children\":[{\"text\":\"Brian Bergstein\"}]}],\"published\":\"2020-02-19T07:00:28-05:00\",\"bodyItems\":[{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eI\\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s2\\\"\\u003en\\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003e less than a decade, computers have become extremely good at diagnosing diseases, translating languages, and transcribing speech. They can outplay humans at complicated strategy games, create photorealistic images, and suggest useful replies to your emails. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433280\"},{\"type\":\"magazine\",\"data\":{\"url\":\"/magazine/2020/03/\",\"title\":\"March/April 2020\",\"cover\":{\"id\":460150,\"url\":\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/files.technologyreview.com/p/pub/images/ma20cover-formag.jpg\",\"path\":\"images/ma20cover-formag.jpg\",\"mimetype\":\"image/jpeg\",\"filesize\":1308438,\"width\":2197,\"height\":3000}},\"key\":\"magazine\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eYet despite these impressive achievements, artificial intelligence has glaring weaknesses. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433281\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eMachine-learning systems can be duped or confounded by situations they haven\u2019t seen before. A self-driving car gets flummoxed by a scenario that a human driver could handle easily. An AI system laboriously trained to carry out one task (identifying cats, say) has to be taught all over again to do something else (identifying dogs). In the process, it\u2019s liable to lose some of the expertise it had in the original task. Computer scientists call this problem \u201ccatastrophic forgetting.\u201d \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433282\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eThese shortcomings have something in common: they exist because AI systems don\u2019t understand causation. They see that some events are associated with other events, but they don\u2019t ascertain which things directly make other things happen. It\u2019s as if you knew that the presence of clouds made rain likelier, but you didn\u2019t know clouds caused rain.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433283\"},{\"type\":\"imageset\",\"data\":{\"layout\":\"inbody\",\"images\":[{\"id\":459535,\"type\":\"static image\",\"url\":\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/files.technologyreview.com/p/pub/images/pic-bareinboim-web1_0.jpg\",\"path\":\"images/pic-bareinboim-web1_0.jpg\",\"width\":3000,\"height\":885,\"alt\":\"Elias Bareinboim\",\"crop\":{\"x\":0,\"y\":0,\"width\":3000,\"height\":885}}],\"caption\":[{\"text\":\"Elias Bareinboim: AI systems are clueless when it comes to causation.\"}]},\"key\":\"433284\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eUnderstanding cause and effect is a big aspect of what we call common sense, and it\u2019s an area in which AI systems today \u201care clueless,\u201d says Elias Bareinboim. He should know: as the director of the new Causal Artificial Intelligence Lab at Columbia University, he\u2019s at the forefront of efforts to fix this problem. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433285\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eHis idea is to infuse artificial-intelligence research with insights from the relatively new science of causality, a field shaped to a huge extent by Judea Pearl, a Turing Award\u2013winning scholar who considers Bareinboim his prot\u00e9g\u00e9. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433286\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eAs Bareinboim and Pearl describe it, AI\u2019s ability to spot correlations\u2014e.g., that clouds make rain more likely\u2014is merely the simplest level of causal reasoning. It\u2019s good enough to have driven the boom in the AI technique known as deep learning over the past decade. Given a great deal of data about familiar situations, this method can lead to very good predictions. A computer can calculate the probability that a patient with certain symptoms has a certain disease, because it has learned just how often thousands or even millions of other people with the same symptoms had that disease. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433287\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eBut there\u2019s a growing consensus that progress in AI will stall if computers don\u2019t get better at wrestling with causation. If machines could grasp that certain things lead to other things, they wouldn\u2019t have to learn everything anew all the time\u2014they could take what they had learned in one domain and apply it to another. And if machines could use common sense we\u2019d be able to put more trust in them to take actions on their own, knowing that they aren\u2019t likely to make dumb errors. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433288\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s1\\\"\\u003eToday\u2019s AI has only a limited ability to infer what will result from a given action. In reinforcement learning, a technique that has allowed machines to master games like chess and Go, a system uses extensive trial and error to discern which moves will essentially cause them to win. But this approach doesn\u2019t work in messier settings in the real world. It doesn\u2019t even leave a machine with \\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003ea general understanding of how it might play other games. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433289\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eAn even higher level of causal thinking would be the ability to reason about why things happened and ask \u201cwhat if\u201d questions. A patient dies while in a clinical trial; was it the fault of the experimental medicine or something else? School test scores are falling; what policy changes would most improve them? This kind of reasoning is far beyond the current capability of artificial intelligence. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433290\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"div\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003ch3 class=\\\"p4\\\"\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s4\\\"\\u003ePerforming miracles\\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003c/h3\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433291\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"The dream of endowing computers with causal reasoning drew Bareinboim from Brazil to the United States in 2008, after he completed a master\u2019s in computer science at the Federal University of Rio de Janeiro. He jumped at an opportunity to study under Judea Pearl, a computer scientist and statistician at UCLA. Pearl, 83, is a giant\u2014\\u003cem\\u003ethe\\u003c/em\\u003e giant\u2014of causal inference, and his career helps illustrate why it\u2019s hard to create AI that understands causality.\"},\"key\":\"433292\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eEven well-trained scientists are apt to misinterpret correlations as signs of causation\u2014or to err in the opposite direction, hesitating to call out causation even when it\u2019s justified. In the 1950s, for example, a few prominent statisticians muddied the waters around whether tobacco caused cancer. They argued that without an experiment randomly assigning people to be smokers or nonsmokers, no one could rule out the possibility that some unknown\u2014stress, perhaps, or some gene\u2014caused people both to smoke and to get lung cancer.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433293\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eEventually, the fact that smoking causes cancer was definitively established, but it needn\u2019t have taken so long. Since then, Pearl and other statisticians have devised a mathematical approach to identifying what facts would be required to support a causal claim. Pearl\u2019s method shows that, given the prevalence of smoking and lung cancer, an independent factor causing both would be extremely unlikely.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433294\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eConversely, Pearl\u2019s formulas also help identify when correlations can\u2019t be used to determine causation. Bernhard Sch\u00f6lkopf, who researches causal AI techniques as a director at Germany\u2019s Max Planck Institute for Intelligent Systems, points out that you can predict a country\u2019s birth rate if you know its population of storks. That isn\u2019t because storks deliver babies or because babies attract storks, but probably because economic development leads to more babies and more storks. Pearl has helped give statisticians and computer scientists ways of attacking such problems, Sch\u00f6lkopf says.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433295\"},{\"type\":\"imageset\",\"data\":{\"layout\":\"inbody\",\"images\":[{\"id\":459536,\"type\":\"static image\",\"url\":\"https://s3.amazonaws.com/files.technologyreview.com/p/pub/images/judeapearl-web1.jpg\",\"path\":\"images/judeapearl-web1.jpg\",\"width\":3000,\"height\":885,\"alt\":\"Judea Pearl\",\"crop\":{\"x\":0,\"y\":0,\"width\":3000,\"height\":885}}],\"caption\":[{\"text\":\"Judea Pearl: His theory of causal reasoning has transformed science.\"}]},\"key\":\"433296\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003ePearl\u2019s work has also led to the development of causal Bayesian networks\u2014software that sifts through large amounts of data to detect which variables appear to have the most influence on other variables. For example, GNS Healthcare, a company in Cambridge, Massachusetts, uses these techniques to advise researchers about experiments that look promising. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433297\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eIn one project, GNS worked with researchers who study multiple myeloma, a kind of blood cancer. The researchers wanted to know why some patients with the disease live longer than others after getting stem-cell transplants, a common form of treatment. The software churned through data with 30,000 variables and pointed to a few that seemed especially likely to be causal. Biostatisticians and experts in the disease zeroed in on one in particular: the level of a certain protein in patients\u2019 bodies. Researchers could then run a targeted clinical trial to see whether patients with the protein did indeed benefit more from the treatment. \u201cIt\u2019s way faster than poking here and there in the lab,\u201d says GNS cofounder Iya Khalil.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433298\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eNonetheless, the improvements that Pearl and other scholars have achieved in causal theory haven\u2019t yet made many inroads in deep learning, which identifies correlations without too much worry about causation. Bareinboim is working to take the next step: making computers more useful tools for human causal explorations.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433299\"},{\"type\":\"quote\",\"data\":{\"quote\":[{\"text\":\"Pearl says AI can\u2019t be truly intelligent until it has a rich understanding of cause and effect, which would enable the introspection that is at the core of cognition.\"}],\"layout\":\"text-col\",\"style\":\"default\"},\"key\":\"433300\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eOne of his systems, which is still in beta, can help scientists determine whether they have sufficient data to answer a causal question. Richard McElreath, an anthropologist at the Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology, is using the software to guide research into why humans go through menopause (we are the only apes that do).\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433301\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eThe hypothesis is that the decline of fertility in older women benefited early human societies because women who put more effort into caring for grandchildren ultimately had more descendants. But what evidence might exist today to support the claim that children do better with grandparents around? Anthropologists can\u2019t just compare the educational or medical outcomes of children who have lived with grandparents and those who haven\u2019t. There are what statisticians call confounding factors: grandmothers might be likelier to live with grandchildren who need the most help. Bareinboim\u2019s software can help McElreath discern which studies about kids who grew up with their grandparents are least riddled with confounding factors and could be valuable in answering his causal query. \u201cIt\u2019s a huge step forward,\u201d McElreath says.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433302\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"div\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003ch3 class=\\\"p4\\\"\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s4\\\"\\u003eThe last mile\\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003c/h3\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433303\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eB\\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eareinboim talks fast and often gestures with two hands in the air, as if he\u2019s trying to balance two sides of a mental equation. It was halfway through the semester when I visited him at Columbia in October, but it seemed as if he had barely moved into his office\u2014hardly anything on the walls, no books on the shelves, only a sleek Mac computer and a whiteboard so dense with equations and diagrams that it looked like a detail from a cartoon about a mad professor. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433304\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eHe shrugged off the provisional state of the room, saying he had been very busy giving talks about both sides of the causal revolution. Bareinboim believes work like his offers the opportunity not just to incorporate causal thinking into machines, but also to improve it in humans. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433305\"},{\"type\":\"cta\",\"data\":{\"layout\":\"left\",\"template\":\"download\"},\"key\":\"433306\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eGetting people to think more carefully about causation isn\u2019t necessarily much easier than teaching it to machines, he says. Researchers in a wide range of disciplines, from molecular biology to public policy, are sometimes content to unearth correlations that are not actually rooted in causal relationships. For instance, some studies suggest drinking alcohol will kill you early, while others indicate that moderate consumption is fine and even beneficial, and still other research has found that heavy drinkers outlive nondrinkers. This phenomenon, known as the \u201creproducibility crisis,\u201d crops up not only in medicine and nutrition but also in psychology and economics. \u201cYou can see the fragility of all these inferences,\u201d says Bareinboim. \u201cWe\u2019re flipping results every couple of years.\u201d \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433307\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eHe argues that anyone asking \u201cwhat if\u201d\u2014medical researchers setting up clinical trials, social scientists developing pilot programs, even web publishers preparing A/B tests\u2014should start not merely by gathering data but by using Pearl\u2019s causal logic and software like Bareinboim\u2019s to determine whether the available data could possibly answer a causal hypothesis. Eventually, he envisions this leading to \u201cautomated scientist\u201d software: a human could dream up a causal question to go after, and the software would combine causal inference theory with machine-learning techniques to rule out experiments that wouldn\u2019t answer the question. That might save scientists from a huge number of costly dead ends.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433308\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eBareinboim described this vision while we were sitting in the lobby of MIT\u2019s Sloan School of Management, after a talk he gave last fall. \u201cWe have a building here at MIT with, I don\u2019t know, 200 people,\u201d he said. How do those social scientists, or any scientists anywhere, decide which experiments to pursue and which data points to gather? By following their intuition: \u201cThey are trying to see where things will lead, based on their current understanding.\u201d \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433309\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eThat\u2019s an inherently limited approach, he said, because human scientists designing an experiment can consider only a handful of variables in their minds at once. A computer, on the other hand, can see the interplay of hundreds or thousands of variables. Encoded with \u201cthe basic principles\u201d of Pearl\u2019s causal calculus and able to calculate what might happen with new sets of variables, an automated scientist could suggest exactly which experiments the human researchers should spend their time on. Maybe some public policy that has been shown to work only in Texas could be made to work in California if a few causally relevant factors were better appreciated. Scientists would no longer be \u201cdoing experiments in the darkness,\u201d Bareinboim said. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433310\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eHe also doesn\u2019t think it\u2019s that far off: \u201cThis is the last mile before the victory.\u201d\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433311\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"div\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003ch3 class=\\\"p4\\\"\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s4\\\"\\u003eWhat if? \\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003c/h3\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433312\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eF\\u003c/span\\u003e\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003einishing that mile will probably require techniques that are just beginning to be developed. For example, Yoshua Bengio, a computer scientist at the University of Montreal who shared the 2018 Turing Award for his work on deep learning, is trying to get neural networks\u2014the software at the heart of deep learning\u2014to do \u201cmeta-learning\u201d and notice the causes of things. \\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433313\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eAs things stand now, if you wanted a neural network to detect when people are dancing, you\u2019d show it many, many images of dancers. If you wanted it to identify when people are running, you\u2019d show it many, many images of runners. The system would learn to distinguish runners from dancers by identifying features that tend to be different in the images, such as the positions of a person\u2019s hands and arms. But Bengio points out that fundamental knowledge about the world can be gleaned by analyzing the things that are similar or \u201cinvariant\u201d across data sets. Maybe a neural network could learn that movements of the legs physically cause both running and dancing. Maybe after seeing these examples and many others that show people only a few feet off the ground, a machine would eventually understand something about gravity and how it limits human movement. Over time, with enough meta-learning about variables that are consistent across data sets, a computer could gain causal knowledge that would be reusable in many domains.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433314\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eFor his part, Pearl says AI can\u2019t be truly intelligent until it has a rich understanding of cause and effect. Although causal reasoning wouldn\u2019t be sufficient for an artificial general intelligence, it\u2019s necessary, he says, because it would enable the introspection that is at the core of cognition. \u201cWhat if\u201d questions \u201care the building blocks of science, of moral attitudes, of free will, of consciousness,\u201d Pearl told me.\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433315\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cspan class=\\\"s3\\\"\\u003eYou can\u2019t draw Pearl into predicting how long it will take for computers to get powerful causal reasoning abilities. \u201cI am not a futurist,\u201d he says. But in any case, he thinks the first move should be to develop machine-learning tools that combine data with available scientific knowledge: \u201cWe have a lot of knowledge that resides in the human skull which is not utilized.\u201d\\u003c/span\\u003e\"},\"key\":\"433316\"},{\"type\":\"html\",\"data\":{\"element\":\"p\",\"innerHTML\":\"\\u003cem\\u003eBrian Bergstein, a former editor at MIT
